Anyone you're spending time with, whether professionally or at home, deserves your full attention. The reality is that the human brain is full of entropy anyway, so even a focused conversation, meeting, or play time is somehow compromised. Actually doing other activities at the same time destroys the human connection.

Patience. For the most part, steering people to draw their own conclusions about things at work is key. Even if it takes longer than just telling them what to do, it produces better results. With kids, patience takes on a whole new meaning but giving them space to work through issues and scenarios on their own, while hard, clearly fosters independence.

Alignment. If you and your senior staff disagree about something, cross‐communication confuses the team. If you and your spouse aren't on the same page about something, just watch those kids play the two of you off each other. A united front at the top is key!

I assume that entrepreneurs who have families have gone through a lot of this chapter's experiences themselves, so I'll close with one very clear and unambiguous piece of advice. If you're a single entrepreneur, make sure you (1) make time for a social life so that someday you can have a partner and/or family, and (2) before you commit to a permanent partnership – and certainly before you decide to have kids – make sure you think long and hard about whether you're ready for the changes that will invariably come and impact you and your business. If you're ready to move ahead, be intentional about changing your work life to make room for home.

Management Moment

Don’t Just Speak – Be Heard

Often, a very clear conversation can end up with two very different understandings down the road. This is the problem I'd characterize as “what gets said isn't necessarily what gets heard.”

Why does this happen? Sometimes, what gets said isn't 100 percent crystal clear. More often than not, this is around delivering bad news – delivering difficult news is hard and not for the squeamish. This problem can be fixed by brute force. If you are giving someone their last warning before firing him, don't mumble something about “not great performance” and “consequences.” Look him in the eye and say, “If you do not do x, y, and z in the next 30 days, you will be fired.”

In other cases, the person on the receiving end of the conversation may want to hear something else. Solving this problem is more challenging. To get around this issue, try asking the person to whom you're speaking to “play back in your own words what you just heard.” See if they get it right. Also send a very clear follow‐up email after the conversation, recapping it and asking for confirmation of receipt.

Sometimes, things get lost in translation. Clarity of message, boldness of approach, and forcing playback and written confirmation are a few ways to close the gap between what gets said and what gets heard.

Chapter 47
Traveling

For my 40th birthday a few years back, my team gave me a poster from FlightMemory.com that plotted all of my air travel for a full 12‐month period on a world map: 34 airports, 90 flights, 157,000 miles. While it was entertaining to know that I circumnavigated the globe 6.3 times that year – and while I am not immune to the perks of getting upgraded for being a frequent flier – that was a very sobering moment!

Business travel is inevitable. I've done a ton of it throughout my career no matter what job I've had. The reality is that no matter how connected the world is, sometimes there's no substitute for showing up for something in person. As CEO, you need to do a lot of this. Even if your company is 100 percent in one location, you are your company's main ambassador, which means you need to be out of the office and meeting with customers, partners, suppliers, and speaking at industry events regularly. All of those are likely to involve some degree of travel, even in the most local of businesses.

Sealing the Deal with a Handshake

While you have to watch expenses, sometimes it's worth traveling 5,000 miles for a five‐minute meeting. I relearned this lesson a few years back. We were negotiating a big deal with a company on the West Coast and we were at a tense and critical spot in the negotiations. I knew that the only way to move the deal forward to a handshake and a term sheet was to meet face to face with the decision makers on the other side of the table, in person. So I got on a plane. It wasn't my first choice of activities; the trip was a long way to go for a really short meeting. Yet it was 100 percent worthwhile, with a very specific mission accomplished.

It's important to be present and accounted for in business settings, and with everyone's busy schedules and increasingly frenzied and multitasking office environments, it's harder than ever to really get someone's attention. There's just no substitute for looking someone in the eye and doing a real handshake, not a virtual one. Of course, you can't travel all the time, so learning how to say no to a potential trip – even if it's a client visit or speaking engagement – is important. There's no formula for this but when you start to know flight attendants on a first‐name basis, that's a good sign that it's time to cut back.

Making the Most of Travel Time

In addition to the mission you're traveling for, there are ancillary benefits to being on the road. There are four things I try to take advantage of while traveling:

Time for project work. If your day is anything like mine, it's pretty full of meetings and interruptions. Once in a while, you have some “actual work” that you have to do, whether it's writing a new investor presentation, mapping out your game plan for next year, thinking through a tough strategic question, reviewing massive amounts of material – or writing chapters of a book about being a startup CEO. Long‐form travel time is a great opportunity for this kind of work.

Time with a colleague. One thing I've always appreciated in my career but have grown even more attached to of late is traveling with colleagues. Any time I have an opportunity to do so, I jump on it. First, I find that I get solid work time in with a colleague in transit: it's a check‐in meeting that isn't rushed with a hard stop, interrupted by the phone or visitors and in person. Second, I find that I get more “creative” work time in with a colleague on a flight, especially a long one. Some of the time that isn't in a structured meeting invariably turns to brainstorming or more idle work chatter. Some great ideas have come out of flights I've taken in the past 20 years! Finally, my colleague and I get more social time than usual on a plane. Social time is an incredibly important part of managing and developing personal connections with employees. Time spent next to each other in the air, in an airport security line or lounge, or in a rental car always lends itself to learning more about what's going on in someone's life. Don't get me wrong: even when I travel with someone from Return Path, we each have some “quiet time” to read, work, sleep, and contemplate life. The work and work‐related aspects of the experience are not to be ignored.

Time for myself. You can see that I value staying fresh, which I do by a combination of taking time for myself and pursuing hobbies outside of work. Depending on your operating system, you can make business travel time a time for this. A few hours of relatively undisturbed time on a plane can be just enough time to watch the Star Wars trilogy on your iPad, read three Harlan Coben novels, or catch up on sleep.

Time to be out of touch. This is the flip side of “time for myself,” meaning that sometimes it's okay, even good, to be a bit out of touch. When you're out of sight, sometimes people at work bother you less. Particularly with international travel, people don't seem to want to call: you're a larger number of time zones off, so people just don't think to reach out. Even if I'm doing email once or twice a day when time permits, it's different than being on top of every little thing at the office. It's a good thing to be out of touch a bit when you're not officially unplugged for a vacation. Your company will be fine.

Staying Disciplined on the Road

When you travel as much as I do, you can't lean on the excuse that you're “out of town” and therefore off the hook when it comes to personal discipline. In terms of managing myself while I'm on the road, I also have a few simple rules that I try to stick to, no matter what.

I take care of myself. I get good sleep. In the winter, I take Airborne when I'm flying, which I've found cuts down on getting colds from my fellow passengers. I stick to my exercise routine religiously. It helps reduce jetlag, plus I have more time to exercise when I'm on the road. I am not picky about hotels and try to stay at places that are inexpensive but I have one solid rule, which is that there has to be a good hotel gym, preferably one that's available at all hours. Sometimes I try running outside, which can be a nice way to see another place, but I hate running in the dark in a strange city, so that doesn't always work. I've also developed a couple of exercise routines that I can do even in a small hotel room: push‐ups, sit‐ups, Pilates. Bringing along Xertubes (www.spri.com) allows me to do a 15‐ to 30‐minute strength workout in a hotel room at any time of day, often without needing to bring along much in the way of workout clothes or shoes.

I squeeze every minute out of each day. Being home comes with lots of things to do: Commuting. Time with family. Downtime in front of a screen. Those things can evaporate when you're on the road. On the road, you can work 15 hours per day, and get in a good dinner, enough sleep, and exercise. Some of my most productive and simultaneously most “me” times are on business trips as a result of squeezing every minute out of the day.

I find time to take some little bit of joy in wherever I am. Dinners out, whether alone or (more likely) with colleagues or clients, are a minimum. For international travel that's particularly tasty, but unless you're doing a lot of travel to Peoria (sorry, Peoria), there's probably some great local cuisine to seek out as well. Especially with international travel, I also try to block out at least a couple of hours to see some local sights. That doesn't always work out but if I've never been to that city or country before, I make an extra effort. I once hired a taxi to give a colleague and me a rapid‐fire tour of Istanbul, and another time a colleague and I who were in Copenhagen took a cab over the bridge to Malmo, Sweden, for a cup of coffee because neither of us had ever been to Sweden before. Hardly a substitute for a vacation but it was better than nothing.

Chapter 48
Taking Stock of the Year

At the end of every year, I take a few minutes to refresh my operating system for the upcoming year. I reflect on how the business is doing, on my goals and development plans, and on what I want to accomplish in the upcoming year. Although most of that work is focused on how to move the business forward, I also make sure to take stock of my own career.

Celebrating “Yes”; Addressing “No”

Whenever I take stock of the past year, I always ask myself four questions:

Am I having fun at work?

Am I learning and growing as a professional?

Is my work financially rewarding enough, either in the short term or in the long term?

Am I having the impact I want to have on the world?

Of course, I always shoot for four yes responses. As long as I get at least two or three yesses, I feel like I'm in good shape and I know which one or two things to work on in the coming year. That's one of the main inputs I have into setting my agenda for the coming year.

Any time the answer to one of those questions is no, I ask why.

Are You Having Fun?

Are the people around me not fun people? Then I am hiring poorly. Are they fun people who aren't having fun currently? Then either they're working too hard or they aren't seeing enough reward or recognition for their efforts and they're frustrated because their efforts aren't going anywhere. Or maybe they aren't well equipped to do the jobs they have to do for any number of reasons. Is everyone around me having fun, except me? Then the problem clearly rests with me.

If I'm not enjoying what I'm doing, then I either need to delegate it, learn to enjoy it, or recognize that if I have to do it and I will never enjoy it, then I either have to tolerate it or find another job!

Are You Learning and Growing as a Professional?

There are a lot of sources of learning and growth that you can and should have as a CEO. I've mentioned some of them in other parts of the book. If your business is changing at all – you're in a dynamic industry, your business is just growing, or you're pivoting or changing directions, for example – then you have a tiger by the tail and are likely to get dragged into learning and growing by the natural momentum of the business. What if it's not? What if your startup is not adding people or changing product direction? What if it's going sideways? Then you need to push yourself to learn and grow on your own.

Dive into books or take a business class. Think about your business's largest problem and go meet with five other people in the world who have run across comparable problems and solved them. If nothing else, spend time writing down the problems you have and work through a root‐cause analysis. Become even more of an expert in some aspect of your business. Any of these things will help you get smarter about what you're doing.

Is It Financially Rewarding?

You may work at a nonprofit or be highly altruistic about your work but my guess is that if you're reading this book, you care about putting your talents to work so that you can make money for yourself and your family to live nicely, both today and in the future. If you're feeling like the financial reward isn't there in your job, it can be extremely demotivating and cause you to check out.

If it's a short‐term problem (e.g., your salary is too low or you missed this year's numbers and didn't get a bonus), then you can attack that directly either by looking closely at your compensation with your board's compensation committee and looking at external comparables, or by making sure you are realistic and fair when you structure next year's incentive compensation program. If you are concerned about long‐term financial rewards, there could be a number of causes, each of which has its own solution. If it's because your business isn't building enough value fast enough, then you're faced with more existential problems of what business you're in or thorny issues about how well you're executing – and now you have your main agenda item for the upcoming year, and maybe that's around selling the company. If it's because you don't own enough equity in your company or if it's because your company's cap table has too much preferred stock sitting in front of your common stock and you're worried that you won't make much money even if your company does well, then you need to discuss that problem openly and honestly with your board or compensation committee, or at least with one trusted director.

Finally, if your needs have changed even though the circumstances of your business haven't, you need to think hard about whether you can change the circumstances of your business, whether it's appropriate for you to have one of the above kinds of conversations with your board, or whether you should be in the job in the first place. It's one thing to be a hero in the first two years of your company and pay yourself $75,000 while you live in a studio apartment at age 25. It's another to do that when you're 35 with two kids and a mortgage.

Are You Making an Impact?

As I mentioned earlier, impact is a broad term and having impact on the world as a CEO doesn't mean that your company has to be developing a cure for a disease. Whether it's family, friends, colleagues, clients, or some slice of humanity, you should be able to have a real impact on others around you. In your role as CEO, the bare minimum impact you should have is on your colleagues. If your company is creating jobs or if you're helping create a great work environment for people, remember that those are both quite impactful on the world around you.

So if you answer no to this last “take stock” question, the problem could be as simple as you not thinking broadly about what impact means. If you truly feel like you and, by extension, your business, aren't having any impact on anyone – that's a sign that you might be in the wrong job!

Long‐Time Startup CEO Bob Blumberg on Living an Integrated Entrepreneurial Life

My dad is my first entrepreneurial hero. He started his company in 1981 and ran it with two massive pivots along the way until he sold it almost 30 years later. I've asked him to share some of his thoughts on how to manage yourself for the long haul. If anyone knows about that, it's him!

Entrepreneurs may start their companies at any age, from their 20s to their 60s. They can be single, married with or without kids, divorced with or without kids – and they could be in any of those family states at any point in their company's growth cycle. The key to making it all work is an unusual combination of self‐discipline, flexibility, and the ability to constantly reprioritize.

At any stage, there will usually be more demands on your time than you can meet. It would be great to have a 27‐hour day, while limiting everyone else to 24. (Maybe there's an invention and a company‐to‐be in that idea!) You can't let your company founder or fall apart while you take care of personal or family matters, but you also can't allow your family or personal life to crumble while you tend to your business needs.

You will work best by anticipating needs and issues and dealing with them before they become crises. Once you fall into a reacting mode, it's difficult to climb back out.

One way to think about pending issues that require your attention is in a 2‐by‐2 matrix – is an issue serious or not‐so‐serious and is it time critical or not? This can help sort out the order of doing things while keeping a focus on the issues that really need your attention, even though they may not yet be crises. Obviously, each issue also has to be given the test of “can all or part of this be delegated?”

If your business succeeds but your marriage or children do not because you haven't put the time into your home life, you have not truly succeeded. If they succeed personally but your business fails, you have not succeeded in that case, either. You must maintain the self‐discipline to do at least the absolute minimum required for your own well‐being, constantly reprioritize where you need to focus attention, and, above all, be able to communicate clearly to those who need to know why you are making the choices that you do. You need to sell your spouse and children on the importance of your work, sell your colleagues on how seriously you take your family responsibilities, and convince yourself that you have to be in top mental and physical condition to accomplish all that is needed – let alone requested – of you.

Bob Blumberg

Founder and CEO Emeritus of SMS Technologies

Part Six
Selling Your Company

When I wrote the first edition of Startup CEO in 2012, we weren't close to an exit yet at Return Path. The second edition comes a few months after the sale of the company to a strategic buyer, Validity. This new section of the book is a fleshedout series of chapters about the sale process, although I will still start it with most of the same chapter that I had in the first edition – that material is still interesting and relevant.

This section deals only with the sale of a company; it does not discuss other financial outcomes like taking a company public or doing a leveraged (or other) recapitalization, which in and of themselves are not exits.

And full disclosure – Return Path is the first company I've ever sold. Having said that, I did live through and work on the sale of Moviefone to AOL in 1999, I've helped many CEOs work through acquisitions of their own companies as a board member or advisor, and we acquired a dozen companies over the years, so I have at least a little bit of experience here beyond the one primary data point.

Chapter 49
A Note on Exits

When I started Return Path in the froth of the Internet boom in 1999, someone told me I should hire a writer to follow me around and record everything that happened in the formative weeks and months of the business. The person actually said to me, “You can publish a book about how quickly you built and sold the business – it will be called Ready, Set, Exit.”

One of the most unfortunate stereotypes about startup CEOs is that we're only in it for the exit: the business equivalent of real estate investors who flip a dozen properties a year. That may describe some startup CEOs but it shouldn't describe you.

There is no such thing as an exit strategy for a great company. There are only growth strategies, customer development strategies, and global expansion strategies. There is a difference between being bought and being sold, which I will discuss in one of the later chapters in this section, but great startups are always ones where the management team focuses on building the business to the best of their abilities, without specific regard or aim for an outcome.

Exits and Liquidity

Tim Miller was the chairman and CEO of Rally Software from 2003 to 2015. Rally Software completed a successful initial public offering while I was writing the first edition of this book and was acquired by CA Technologies in 2015. Since the beginning Tim was singularly focused on making Rally Software a great company. Rally and Return Path are companies that have been leading parallel lives in the Boulder, Colorado, area for years and Tim and I frequently compare notes. Like all great entrepreneurs, Tim's focus has been on growth rather than exits, but that doesn't mean he hasn't given the latter considerable thought.

For a startup entrepreneur, a strategic sale represents a life‐altering opportunity to build a personal balance sheet that provides a safety net for career and family. First‐time entrepreneurs in this scenario often have little or no assets in the bank – other than equity in the company. As a result, many people simply pick a sale price that satisfies a financial goal and then proceed to focus on reaching that valuation. This “number” is a useful goal to set and should be reevaluated annually as the business progresses. When the thrilling opportunity arises to be able to sell for the “number,” it's hard to argue against selling the business, putting the money in the bank, and using it to fund your next startup. Once you have got that level of safety and security, you can take another run at building something truly exceptional.

Determining the right number isn't always easy. My advice is to think about the company in terms of a balance sheet number versus an income statement number. Let's look at a very simple income statement example. A company generating 5 percent free cash flow per year from total revenue of $10 million annually at a steady state can generate $1.5 million in cash over a three‐year period. However, analyzing the assets in a balance sheet approach shows that the $10 million run rate business could potentially be sold for $10 million, even at a modest 1x price/sales multiple. The question you have to ask is which would you prefer from a financial perspective: $500,000 per year with all the risk associated with running a going concern or $10 million in cash that you could put in the bank?

In the meantime, remain laser‐focused on growing the company; it will compound in value as it grows. In the preceding example, the $10 million run rate business growing at 100 percent per year could generate $500,000 in year one, $1 million in year two, $2 million in year three, and so on. In this compound growth example, the cash for shareholders would be $3.5 million after three years. If the company were sold for a 1x price/sales multiple in year three, the sale would be worth $40 million.

There are several factors to consider when contemplating a sale. First of all, there will almost always be a better price/sales multiple if the buyer initiates the conversation about acquisition. They will most likely have a more optimistic perspective on the company if they discover the business through a strategic focus on building their company, as opposed to reacting to a company that's looking to sell. Great outcomes are bought, not sold.

The question is how to position the company to be bought for a higher multiple. First and foremost, grow and invest in the business for the long term. There are no shortcuts to building a truly valuable business. Also, make sure to build relationships with strategic partners who might be potential buyers. If and when they come to talk about a potential sale, engage in the conversation so they know about your company but consider a sale only if they offer a significant premium to the true balance sheet value of the company. The premium for a 100 percent growth company would, of course, be much larger than the premium for a 10 percent growth company – so grow, grow, grow.

Of course, a sale isn't the only successful outcome for a startup. The other is to remain a long‐term stand‐alone company and eventually go public.

The downsides of being under public investor scrutiny are outweighed by the upsides of continuing to run a successful company. The only way to reach that potential is to scale and grow at a rate of at least 25 to 30 percent annually. When growth slips, so does the likelihood of an eventual initial public offering.

Given the active M&A market for emerging growth companies, it is extremely difficult to build a long‐term stand‐alone company, especially in the tech sector. The best way to reduce the chances of an unwanted sale while maximizing shareholder value is to grow at the fastest rate possible. In that case, only a premium valuation would cause shareholders to opt for a strategic sale.

However, these two outcomes aren't mutually exclusive. It's always good practice to consider the possibility of an acquisition before or even after an IPO. Regardless of whether you eventually sell, go public, or do both, the imperative for startup CEOs remains the same: grow!

Tim Miller

Former CEO, Rally Software

Chapter 50
Preparing Yourself for an Exit

As I said earlier in the book, entrepreneurship is full of extreme highs and extreme lows, and the thing you have to get used to is when they happen at the same time. Nowhere in your startup CEO journey will you find this to be truer than during the process of selling your company.

How Do You Know It's Time to Sell?

This is a tricky question. There's an old adage that “great companies are bought, not sold.” I don't quite agree with that wording. Certainly, great companies, those “lightning in a bottle” rocket ships, are likely to attract proactive offers from strategic buyers early and often. And it's also true that weak, financially unhealthy, or more run‐of‐the‐mill companies may never attract a proactive offer. But sometimes great companies don't have an obvious natural buyer, or the timing of wanting or needing to sell a company doesn't line up with the needs of the market.

Regardless, the question stands: How do you know it's time to sell your company? There are a few times that point to the answer of “could be now”:

You receive an inbound, unsolicited offer that is very exciting to you, your team, and your shareholders, either because the number is disproportionately large relative to where you believe the company should be valued today, or because the buyer and the opportunity of the combination of the two companies is exciting. Inbound offers are in some ways the easiest to get your arms around. My long‐time friend and board member, Fred Wilson, always reminds me, “At every price, you are either a buyer or a seller.” So, if you get an unsolicited offer and you like the buyer, you are either deciding to sell the company at that price, or you are deciding that, at that price, you are willing to buy the company.

You believe the company is at its most likely high watermark of valuation within a two‐plus‐year forward‐looking horizon. This isn't necessarily a must‐sell time, but in the world of “buy low, sell high,” it certainly could be. Examples of this might be when you think your market is starting to tap out over the next couple of years; when you are staring down a massive amount of new investment or reinvention/transformation in order to maintain or expand your company's market position and revenue; or when you have just completed some major internal house cleaning, whether of technology, staff, or something else. None of these times is necessarily a signal that the company is in trouble. There may well be a higher peak off in the future. But at a major inflection point like these, you owe it to yourself and your shareholders to consider whether it's the right time to sell.

You have a major internal problem on your hands related to the cap table. For example, you might have a founder who owns a significant amount of stock who either needs liquidity or whom you need to push out of the company. Or you might have a major institutional investor who requires liquidity for some reason. Neither of these is a must‐sell situation; there are other ways to buy out individual shareholders or create an equity or debt financing event around that outcome. But again, this is a major inflection point at which it makes sense to consider a sale, especially if the cap table problem is large enough. A lack of alignment of financial interests around the board table, or around the management team, can slowly eat away at the leadership morale of the company (or even just you) if it's not addressed.

If you sense that one of these situations is coming, it's time to start preparing yourself and your company for an exit.

Mental Preparation

Just because it's the right time for a sale of the business, or a sale is likely in the near future, doesn't mean you automatically become mentally prepared for such a massive change to your own life. If you're a founder entrepreneur who has led your company for several years, the odds are you have a significant amount of emotional investment in your company. For many entrepreneurs, the company is a deeply embedded part of their identity as a human – right or wrong, for better or for worse. The first thing to do when starting the personal, mental journey of selling your company is to recognize these things, for yourself, but even to acknowledge them out loud to your significant other or close friends or members of your management team (more on them in a minute).

For me, one of my self‐management tools discussed in Chapter 48, “Taking Stock of the Year,” came in very handy as I prepared myself to sell Return Path. Those four questions that I ask myself at the end of every year:

Am I having fun at work?

Am I learning and growing as a professional?

Is my work financially rewarding enough, either in the short term or in the long term?

Am I having the impact I want to have on the world?

They all played a key role in my preparation. I realized that the answers to these questions weren't as strong as they once were, and in fact that the answer to some of them could be more emphatic if I were doing something else. Now that in and of itself isn't a reason to sell a company, of course, but for me that realization was a moment that reinforced my belief that the decision to sell the company was good for the business – and for me personally.

There's a major component to your mental preparation that also revolves around answering the question, “Am I ready to let go?” Of course, there are many different types of company sale transactions, but invariably all of them are likely to lead to a lot less control over the business. Even if you sell the company to a financial buyer and are still running it as CEO, you may find yourself with a lot less authority and autonomy than you did if you had a more diversified shareholder base and board, or of course if you had no institutional investors and simply founders and angel investors. Selling to a strategic buyer means your company might not even exist anymore; even if it's a business unit or a product line in a larger organization, it won't be an independent company or even close.

So you need to think deeply about the different range of personal outcomes and get your head around what those outcomes would mean for you, whether you'd be interested in them, and why or under what circumstances. Some entrepreneurs I know have sold their companies to strategic buyers, gone on to be effectively product managers at larger companies, and are thrilled – thrilled that they are able to lighten their personal burdens mentally, thrilled that they can really see “their baby” grow up and thrive and succeed in a new environment, thrilled that they have more time in their lives beyond work. Some entrepreneurs I know have sold their companies to private equity firms and maintained their roles as CEO, delighted to have a single voice around the board table instead of five, and happy to have taken money off the table while still having a second bite at the proverbial apple.

But for every one of those two types of happy entrepreneurs, my guess is that there are two unhappy ones, or at least two who should be preparing themselves to leave their company after a transaction and not pretend that they're going to be happy relinquishing that much control over their company and still being there to execute the vision or financial guidelines of other people. There's nothing wrong with coming up with the answer of “I am not sure, but I'd like to give it a try.” But there is a problem – mostly for yourself, but it will spread to others as well – if you really don't want to be at the company after a sale and a proper transition, but you force it for whatever reason. That will not end well for anyone.

There is no right or wrong answer here. But there probably is a right or wrong answer for you. That's the most important thing to think through, deeply, at the early stages of working on selling your company.

Brace for Impact

Besides the mental preparation involved with thinking about life after a transaction, you also need to prepare yourself for the sale process itself. Even the easiest ones (and there's no such thing as an easy one) take longer than you think, are more exhausting than you imagined possible, involve a lot of drama with buyers and lawyers, and invariably involve a large number of moments where you get bad news.

The bad news could be a potential buyer saying no. It could be a buyer retrading the terms of a deal that you thought were settled. It could be mid‐level people at a buyer nitpicking some area of your business to death with questions or concerns that don't make sense to you. All of them will feel like a prospective client rejection or a prospective rejection. Your baby has been called ugly.

But regardless of the nature and volume of these, I can promise you they will happen and will be unpleasant. So buckle up!

Bringing Your Family Along for the Ride

At the advice of several friends, by the time we sold the company in 2019, I had already laid a lot of groundwork for that with my kids, who were 10, 11, and 12 at the time. The reality is that my kids were younger than Return Path, so to them, my identity and the company's identity were completely fused together. Not only that, but kids don't understand startup economics and equity, so the thought of “Daddy being unemployed” is scary to them. As my friends who had been through this process before advised me, contemplating a life transition like this might be hard for me, but it would be 10 times more difficult for kids!

Two years before we actually sold the company, I started a dialog with the kids about how something like that would probably happen someday. Once we got past them being upset (and they were very upset for a while), they got more and more comfortable with the concept. When we were in the final deal process, they even started to get excited and ask me questions every night about how due diligence was going, and whether I was sure I was getting the best deal possible. Once the transaction closed, all the unhappiness and fear were gone, and they were able to be as excited as I was about it.

Chapter 51
Preparing Your Company for an Exit

While you clearly need to spend some time preparing yourself mentally for the sale of your company, you need to spend even more time preparing your company. There are really two areas to dive into on this topic: how you handle knowledge and communications around the topic internally, and how you actually prepare for a transaction.

Private Discussions versus Public Discussions

The first decision you'll need to make around sharing the news of a pending sale of the company internally is how broadly you want to share the news. There are two primary factors that can influence your decision:

How many people need to know in order to successfully complete the transaction?

What is your organization's culture? How transparent are you about big and potentially scary things?

In terms of the first question, there's no set answer. It depends how large your company is and who the buyer is. Sometimes deals can be done in a weekend with founders only; others can take up to six months and require extensive participation on the part of dozens of team members at multiple levels in the organization.

Regardless of culture and norms, you do need to consider this: How disruptive is the news of a pending sale likely to be to employees? To customers? Even if you have a culture of fairly extreme transparency, the sale of the company is one thing that I think most people would expect to be kept fairly confidential. But a culture of transparency and trust may lead you to share this information with a broader group than required to complete the transaction.

In making this decision, you have to balance authenticity and transparency with being smart about internal communications. Deals fall through all the time. Don't overcommunicate too early or you risk sending the message that you're looking for an exit when all you were doing was exploring an option. And it probably goes without saying that you as CEO need to keep running your business, fully engaged every day, until the ink is dry, the money is in the bank, and the lawyers tell you the deal is closed.

Once you've made a decision about how wide the “circle of trust” is going to be on this news, your next step is to figure out how to communicate the news to your team in a way that both brings them along for the ride in terms of the decision and process and also makes it clear to them what role they will be playing in it. Is the actual decision to sell something that's still not 100 percent made, and they'll be part of that? Will they have a voice in who to sell to if there are multiple interested parties? Do you just need them to participate in due diligence and documentation? Again, there's no right or wrong answer here, other than that clarity between you and your team on the specifics is critical to a successful outcome.

How do you best bring people on your team along for the ride? We had a couple of sale processes over the years (obviously some did not come to a conclusion along the way), and each time, I engaged my inner circle of senior executives in the thinking process before engaging our board so they were in on the decision from the ground floor. And once our board and I had decided to proceed with a process, I created essentially a slide deck that walked the audience through the decision from front to back, including directly addressing the “what's in it for me” question. I used that deck, in modified versions over time, every time we brought a new team member into the process.

Regardless of how many people in the company know a sale is on the horizon and how many times you swear people to confidentiality, you must expect and prepare for both internal and external leaks. They'll really disappoint and anger you, but they will happen. And again, regardless of how committed you are to transparency as a value, once you've committed to the right in‐the‐know group, unless you're prepared to rethink that, you need to develop good responses to leak questions. Both internal and external audiences can be handled with a similar response along the lines of “We are asked regularly if we are interested in selling the company, and we owe it to ourselves to always take those inquiries seriously.” And beyond that, “No comment.” There's not a lot more you can do unless you're prepared to flat‐out lie to people who ask! And to be clear, if there's ever a time to do that in your startup CEO journey, this might be it, since the leak could put the deal and/or the company at risk.

Also, regardless of how many people in the company know a sale is on the horizon, the thing you have to keep top of mind in every conversation is that the person to whom you are speaking is likely silently asking, “What's in it for me?” as he or she is politely listening to your monologue on what's about to happen and why. Once you realize this, you'll greatly inform your internal talking points, and you may well reach an equilibrium where you do a mix of group communications and individual communications, since the impact of a transaction on one person might be very different from the impact of the same transaction on someone else. Each person will have different rewards and risks as part of the deal, including the risk of not having a job. Even if you are uncertain as to outcomes – you have no idea what the price will be, or you have no idea if a buyer is likely to want to keep Person X versus Person Y – at least putting these issues out on the table early and clearly starts to destigmatize the process and build or maintain trust between you and your colleagues.

Due Diligence and Disclosure Schedules

By the time you prepare to sell your company, these two phrases are likely familiar to you, as you've gone through them in any financing you've done along the way. But they take on a whole new meaning when you move from selling a minority interest in your company to selling the whole thing. For those who haven't been through even a financing yet, due diligence just means “buyers are learning everything they can learn about all aspects of the company,” and disclosure schedules are the scores of pages at the end of the sale document where you have to list out hundreds to thousands of granular details about your business.

Both, at best, take a lot of time and painstaking attention to detail. At worst, they can be the bane of your and your team's existence for weeks or months on end. You can do a number of things ahead of or at the beginning of a sale process to make your lives easier, although it's almost impossible to anticipate everything a buyer will ask for or require ahead of time. Here are some things you can do:

First, get a full and complete due diligence checklist from your lawyer before you start a sale process. Even though your potential buyers may have different lists, they aren't likely to be all that different. Think of this as your diligence and disclosure roadmap, and by having this early, you can start people internally working on organizing things at the front end of the process. Especially when it comes to corporate, legal, and financial items, which can be exhaustive and cumbersome to produce, you'll be happy you have a head start on this (see the Management Moment sidebar below). All of these things will have to end up in some kind of shared online folder or data room at some point, so the earlier you start down that road, the better.

Second, work with your CFO on organizing the company's financials, key operating metrics, and customer/revenue detail early. You will likely have a ton of questions coming at you about these over time. The more comprehensive your models and spreadsheets are (both backward looking and forward looking), the easier your life will be.

Third, start working on your management presentation early. Typically, you will need two versions of a slide deck to tell the story of your business. The first one will be relatively short (15–25 slides) and look a lot like an investor pitch deck and tells the story at a high level. The second one could go into hundreds of pages, depending on the size and complexity of your business, and is designed to answer almost any question you are likely to be asked in a live due diligence meeting with potential buyers. Typically, the shorter one is a subset of the longer one, although you may need to combine some slides and concepts from the long deck into the short one, not just cut slides. In order to do this work, you'll need to get each functional leader in your business to prepare a series of slides to run through his or her area of the company – the qualitative story (what we do to contribute to the mission), financial numbers or operating metrics, organization, what's unique, opportunities, challenges, and so on. Different areas will require different slides and concepts.

Finally, make sure you are coaching each functional leader of your business on how to tell his or her part of the story. You will certainly be part of due diligence sessions with potential buyers and will be the first responder to many or even most questions in those meetings, but unless your company is still very early stage, you are probably not the best equipped person to answer detailed questions in every area of the business. You also want to showcase your team – let them participate in the process, learn from it, get to know potential buyers, and shine.

Begin with the End in Mind

While at the beginning of this section I joked about the “ready, set, exit” mentality that some founders can have, there's one thing I wish I had done from the outset of starting Return Path, and that is having the end of a sale in mind from day one. I don't mean that in terms of how you build the business – your strategy, products, and the like, although that may be true for some people as well.

But the advice in this chapter of “get a full and complete due diligence checklist from your lawyer before you start a sale process” is something I wish I had done the day I started the company! Knowing what things buyers will want to see, in what form, and how well organized, would have influenced me and my CFO to be more orderly about corporate records (things like shareholder votes and board minutes) as well as client contracts. It's not that we were disorganized, but over 20 years we put things in several different places and didn't always migrate old records to new systems. When it came time to put together due diligence and load things into the data room, it was a lot more complicated than it needed to be.

Chapter 52
The Sale Process

So, you've committed to selling the company. You've begun the long and winding journey of mentally preparing yourself for the event. You've prepared your company and team for it. Unfortunately, all of that work is the pregame show. Now it's time for the game.

The Arcs of a Deal

While no deal follows a direct or consistent path, there are two general arcs of a transaction that you can anticipate, depending on the situation's dynamics.

If your sale process starts with an inbound offer that you expand to engage other potential buyers to generate competition, the process will look something like this:

Inbound interest from Buyer 1 leading to nonbinding offer from Buyer 1. This goes by several different names: term sheet, LOI (letter of intent), IOI (indication of interest), LOMU (letter of mutual understanding). These will all ask you to sign some level of “no shop,” or exclusivity. You will try to avoid doing that while working on the next two items in parallel.

In parallel, you will engage in basic due diligence with Buyer 1 while feverishly working to generate similar interest and LOIs from Buyers 2, 3, and 4.

Compare offers, settle on a winner, and sign an exclusive term sheet.

In parallel, engage in deep due diligence and deal documentation with the buyer serving up deal documentation.

Sign and close (sometimes simultaneously, sometimes not).

If your sale process is a sell‐side process, meaning you determine that you want to sell the company without a buyer in hand and hire a banker to generate a process, the process will look something like this:

Hire banker.

Do as much pregame work as possible, as outlined in the prior chapter, from setting up the data room to creating your management presentations.

Bankers do waves of outreach to potential buyers; you do a bunch of initial presentations.

Bankers narrow down lists of buyers to the most serious ones; you and your team do a handful of the “long” presentation.

Bankers and lawyers present your deal documentation and ask final bidders to provide a near‐final markup of deal docs with their LOIs.

Bankers round up multiple LOIs, you compare offers, settle on a winner, and sign an exclusive term sheet.

Similar to above – engage in final diligence and documentation, sign, and close.

The way I've presented them above, the second process feels a little cleaner and more certain, but that is probably misleading. Both types of process are messy, and nothing ever goes as planned!

Principals versus Outside Advisers

Different types of buyers breed different deal dynamics but remember this: big strategic buyers and all financial buyers are in the business of buying companies – they do tens of transactions per year and hundreds over the long haul. In all likelihood, you will sell your company precisely once. Obviously for a large and important transaction, you will want to use a good lawyer. But it is not at all a sign of weakness to engage a banker, either. Otherwise, you'll be like the guy or gal who brings a knife to a gunfight.

Your lawyer – or a combination of external counsel and internal counsel if you're big enough – will principally work on three things. One is deal documentation: running the documents, markups, and conducting a good percentage of your negotiations on legal points. Two is working on legal due diligence and the often painful disclosure schedules. And three is advising you and your board on key issues around your fiduciary responsibilities, shareholder‐related laws, and process. Even though at the end of the day you (or your board) – not your lawyer – make the decisions, this is a critical time to listen really carefully to your lawyer's advice. It's best to work with your normal outside corporate counsel, who presumably knows your business and shareholders and directors well already. If for some reason your normal outside counsel does not have a lot of experience with transactions, you will still want your counsel in the mix, but you will want to engage additional counsel who does, unless you have a rock star banker who can fill in the gaps.

As noted above, the principal roles of a banker are to help generate awareness and interest on the part of potential buyers; and to help negotiate final terms, especially price and a couple of other material terms like escrows and indemnifications. These roles can take different forms and several different layers of depth, depending on your situation. Even if you have a great inbound offer from your dream buyer and can make a couple of phone calls to generate other interest, you are well served by having a banker to give you some air cover, be the occasional bad guy, generate even more interest, express interest in process details in a more authentic way than you can yourself at times, and keep parties and process moving forward. If you are running a sell‐side process, especially with an eye toward financial buyers, there's no way you will be able to manage the process, let alone have access to, all the right people at all the right firms. Coming back to the “knife at a gun fight” analogy above, can you do a deal without a banker? Sure. Should you? Well, 90–95 percent of the time, no, for mid‐sized or larger deals. For small deals, especially if you have a strong lawyer, investor, or board member who has done lots of transactions, you can probably get away without one.

The most important things you as a principal can do while all this is happening are to keep calm and run the business, negotiate the most important terms, and maintain regular friendly contact with the principal on the other side of the table. I always prefer super‐detailed term sheets; the more of the hard conversations you can front load, the smoother the actual deal documentation process goes. (If you are in a sale process and read this book a long time ago, you might want to refer back to Chapter 22, “When and How to Raise Money,” especially the section called “The Top 11 Takeaways for Financing Negotiations” because the two types of transactions have a lot in common.) And any time your outside advisers hit a wall with the buyer's outside advisers, the two principals will have to jump in and solve the problem.

The result is that you and the other principal will form an odd, very intense partnership out of nowhere, unless it's someone you happen to know or have worked with in the past. That's entirely possible, especially in the case of strategic buyers. Although even then, it's likely you haven't worked extensively with the deal person at the strategic buyer even if your company has a business relationship there. In many cases, the relationship is nothing but temporary scaffolding; once the deal ends, you may have little or no contact with that person ever again. You need to simultaneously be the person's friend and the person's nonadversarial adversary. But above all, until your deal closes, you always need to be investing in the relationship and selling.

Optimizing for Different Stakeholders

In August 2019 as I was writing the second edition of this book, a group of a couple hundred Fortune 1000 CEOs who are members of the Business Roundtable published a set of ethical guidelines for companies that spoke about the importance of not just optimizing value for shareholders, but for all stakeholders, including employees, customers, suppliers, and the community. This isn't unlike the definition of fiduciary responsibilities for Benefit or B Corporations chartered in Delaware.

If you're not a B Corp or someone who signed the Business Roundtable guidelines, you aren't under any legal obligation to optimize the value of a sale to anyone other than shareholders (and at that, in some respects technically just common shareholders). Even if you don't have to optimize for other stakeholders, you might want to, out of respect for your employees or your company's values. Or you might want to optimize the price for shareholders, but then spend time optimizing for employees and other stakeholders within that framework. One good thing about giving out stock options to all your employees is that it can be a good way to align the interests of shareholders with those of employees. But not all companies give all employees equity; and even when that is the case, the economic value of a transaction to employees may be dwarfed by other considerations related to their employment.

And that is frequently “the rub” in selling your company. Almost all of the time, your new owner, whether strategic or financial, will terminate some employees post‐deal. The damage may be small, or it may also be massive. But while you do have to recognize that once a deal is done, you probably no longer have control over the situation – including whether the new owner retains or fires employees – there is a lot you can do in the context of a deal to navigate that. For example, when we sold Return Path to Validity, we insisted on a term in the purchase agreement that Validity had to honor Return Path's historical severance practices, which were far more generous than industry norms. We spelled them out in a very detailed way. This was a negotiation point – and it almost certainly cost our shareholders a little bit of money in the deal – but it was a “must have” for me and the rest of our management team and board because we knew a number of employees were going to be at risk post‐closing. We also negotiated with our board to set aside a portion of the sale proceeds for employee sale bonuses (sometimes called a “carve‐out” or management incentive plan) because we realized that the value of stock options for some employees wasn't going to feel like enough given their levels, length of service, or performance, in the context of this transaction. I think all of these things were appreciated by our team in the end.

Having said all of that, don't forget that optimizing the economic value of the transaction for shareholders is probably at the top of your list for a deal, even if the list has more than one item. The more interested parties you have, the better. I've seen companies literally double or triple the deal price that way. And also, don't forget that there are other strategies for improving or securing the deal value that may be easy for the other side to give on other than price. For example, although this only works if you're being acquired by a public company for stock, when we sold Moviefone to AOL back in the late 1990s, our founders brilliantly took advantage of AOL's bravado at the time and wrote a deal with a collar (i.e., if their stock price dropped below a certain level, they would have to guarantee the price of the deal by adding shares to it) but no cap (i.e., if their stock price kept going up, they wouldn't claw back the number of shares, so we'd just benefit with a higher share price). The net of that negotiation was that Moviefone's shareholders got almost 50 percent more out of the deal than expected at signing!

Coping with a Failed Deal

In the years before we eventually sold Return Path, we went through two different sale processes, both of which fell through. One wasn't so bad – it was a quick “market check” of the company's valuation that was precipitated by a soft offer from one of our shareholders to buy out the others – and it came at a time when I knew the company was not ready for a variety of reasons and therefore unlikely to generate strong offers. We invested some company time in the process but not a lot, and when we finally shut it down, it wasn't such a big deal internally for those who were in the know, and there weren't too many people who even knew.

The other failed process was quite different. It began with an unsolicited inbound offer from a well‐known strategic buyer in the space. With the help of a banker, we were able to parlay that into a superior offer from another well‐known strategic buyer. We had completed exhaustive due diligence and final deal documentation. About 80 people inside the company were in the know, and that group had gotten very excited about the deal. Both our lawyers and the other side's lawyers had signed off on the final deal documentation. Of my executive team, half the group had received their new offers to join the buyer and stay with the company (of course, everyone got a raise and a huge new equity grant); I had delivered the bad news to the other half of the executives that they were going to be let go once the deal closed. And then at the eleventh hour, literally the day before the scheduled signing of the definitive agreement, the buyer got cold feet and backed out. The patient was dead, and there was no way to revive him.

Two days later, at the all‐hands meeting that had been somewhat hastily scheduled to announce the deal internally, my exec team and I (dazed and confused!) found ourselves scrambling to pretend it was just another quarterly hands meeting to review business results.

Fortunately, Return Path had a lot of organizational resilience. But picking up the pieces from this was not an easy undertaking. How did we do it?

We pulled all the execs into a two‐day offsite to allow for a lot of catharsis and emotion and a postmortem.

We took several steps to shore up management's future employment and compensation, from changing employment terms around severance and acceleration, and adding equity grants.

We spent a lot of time working to clarify the operating plan for the business as well as our likely exit paths, now that the stage had been set for a sale and most of us had done all of our mental preparation (management and board alike).

I pushed to obtain commitment from my executive team.

I made sure all of us had a vacation on the books in the very short term so each of us could regroup mentally.

We held similar discussions with the rest of the team who was in the know.

Even with all of that, there was some lasting damage done by the deal's collapse. We lost several executives. We lost many nights' sleep. We lost momentum with our board and shareholders. The business itself might not have suffered, but the executive team certainly did. In the end, whether your deal is at the term sheet stage or the final moments before a closing, until it's done it can be killed or retraded. Pick your favorite analogy (Yogi Berra, or the one about the opera being over), but don't ever forget that.

Brian Andersen and Mark Greenbaum of LUMA Partners on Exit Considerations

Brian Andersen and Mark Greenbaum from LUMA Partners were our outside advisers when we sold Return Path, and were also engaged the two times in prior years when we almost sold the company (see sidebar above, “Coping with a Failed Deal”). Brian has been an investment banker for a decade and prior to that was a senior corporate development executive in Silicon Valley and is the co‐founder and head of digital marketing for LUMA. Mark has been an investment banker for over two decades at Morgan Stanley, GCA, and LUMA, and leads M&A execution for LUMA.

“Companies are bought, not sold.”

This phrase has appeared a number of times in this book – and is constantly stated among M&A practitioners, board members, and startup executives. However, there are two main misconceptions about this phrase that should be clarified:

The phrase should be modified to “Companies that receive a premium valuation are bought, not sold.”

Being “bought” is active, not passive.

Before we dive into both these misconceptions, let's first back up and discuss two critically important topics: (1) What drives valuation, and (2) how do strategic buyers acquire companies? For valuation, we see four main drivers:

Growth: Faster growth = higher multiple. A rule of thumb we use for scaled SaaS businesses is that the forward revenue growth divided by five should approximate the revenue multiple (e.g., a business growing at 40% over the next 12 months would be valued at 8x revenue).

Predictability: More predictability = higher multiple. This is one reason high retention/low churn subscription businesses are valued so richly: knowing their future revenues with high certainty allows for better planning of capital expenditures, hiring, raising and servicing debt, and so on. Conversely, businesses that lack this predictability (e.g., ad networks that have to sell and deliver within each quarter) are valued at lower multiples.

Operating leverage: As a company scales, what portion of each new revenue dollar flows to operating income? For SaaS businesses with high retention rates, revenues from new contracts stack on top of existing revenues, and the operating expenses as a percent of revenues decrease significantly as the business leverages its relatively fixed product development resources. In contrast, a consulting firm that bills clients based on hourly rates has mostly variable costs of production, and thus can only grow its revenues by proportionally increasing expenses – and therefore has very little operating leverage. This is why consulting firms are typically valued at much lower (∼1–2x) revenue multiples than software businesses.

Strategic value: This is the M&A “X Factor” – the value of the target unique to the specific buyer. This is the 1+1=3 dynamic. As an example, would you pay $1 billion for a company with no revenues and a dozen employees? Not likely. But Facebook did for Instagram, and many analysts now estimate the value of the business to be over $100 billion. That value creation was unique to Facebook, given its unique ability to acquire, retain, and monetize users. Since strategic value is specific to each deal and each potential buyer, it is critical for a seller to understand the drivers of strategic value in order to optimize its ability to extract some of this value in the negotiations.

Now let's look at the process of how successful strategic buyers with experienced teams buy companies. These buyers tend to deploy a logical and continuous M&A process that includes: (1) constantly evaluating their business compared to its competitors to identify gaps, (2) evaluating industry trends and opportunities that could enable new growth opportunities, (3) refining the vision of where they want to take their business, and (4) developing a strategy of how they will get there (build versus buy versus partner). Critical capabilities that would take too long to build or are too important to address through partnership drive the company's acquisition roadmap. As capabilities are prioritized, the strategy or corporate development or business teams (it varies among different buyers which group has this responsibility) meet with the potential target companies with these capabilities and assess them against their acquisition criteria, which typically include:

Products (features, functionality)

Technology (architecture, scalability, on‐premises versus software‐as‐a‐service, etc.)

Team (executive team and breakdown of functional areas)

Go‐to‐market (enterprise sales, channel sales, etc.)

Pricing models (license, subscription, freemium, volume‐based, etc.)

Uniqueness (scarcity and defensibility of offering, moats, IP protection, etc.)

Location (where teams are based, where customers are based)

Financials (revenue, revenue growth, margins, profitability, etc.)

Valuation expectations

For strategic deals proper target selection tends to be the biggest driver of success (more so than trying to optimize valuation). So, after identifying the desired target and performing enough “preliminary diligence” to determine that they want to make an offer, the strategic buyer will typically proactively and preemptively make an offer. The summary above describes a company being “bought.”

As an example of how a strategic buyer approaches acquisitions, note how Eric Roza, the former SVP and GM of Oracle's Data Cloud (and founder and CEO of Datalogix, which Oracle acquired for over $1 billion), responded when asked how startup CEOs should interact with strategic buyers:

To date, every acquisition done…was extremely thesis driven. None of them were opportunistic where somebody reached out and said "Hey, we are selling the company; do you want to take a look?“ It happens a lot at Oracle, and we virtually never engage. We just don't have time for it. So, they tend to be a thesis that we are thinking about that is playing out, we have gotten to know the company well, seen them execute, in many cases over years.

As Eric's comments highlight, “being bought” is not a passive activity. In each acquisition he was involved in, he had personally developed a relationship with that company's leaders and witnessed how the business performed over a period of time.

At LUMA Partners, we consistently advise startup CEOs to identify their likely potential buyers and to proactively develop relationships with them to ensure they are properly considered as these buyers evaluate and prioritize targets. A commercial partnership can be an ideal start since business development may lead to corporate development. Partnerships can enable buyers to gain a deeper understanding of a target's products, teams, and the value of the company's offering to joint customers. But just as important is building the personal relationship between the startup CEO (and likely the executive team) and the strategic buyer executive team. Our partner Terry Kawaja consistently states, “Companies don't buy companies; people buy companies.” This is so true. For an acquisition to progress, an “executive sponsor” within the buyer needs to step up and say, “I want to do this deal” and be willing to sign up for the financial performance of the target and take responsibility for the acquired business post‐transaction. Without personal sponsorship within the buyer, no deal. So, relationship building is key.

So far, we have covered the steps a company can proactively take to maximize the chances of being “bought” by a strategic buyer, thus unlocking a premium, strategic valuation. We do appreciate that there are times when companies desire to be “sold,” and therefore decides to run a sell‐side process. These processes typically have the kinds of steps that Matt outlines elsewhere in this section.

We noted above how buyers approach acquisitions and classic valuation drivers, but it is also important to understand a buyer's mindset when considering a target sourced in reaction to a sell‐side process versus approaching a target proactively. When I (Brian) ran corporate development at Interwoven and Omniture, we would be contacted a few times a week by companies overtly trying to sell. We would track these processes and found that the most common outcome was a failed process (no sale), and the second‐most common outcome was a sale at a below‐market valuation. Buyers understand these dynamics, so if a buyer does participate in a sell‐side process, its typical bidding strategy is to put in the lowest possible offer it feels will be considered, and then move up only if they believe there is true competition for the asset. Contrast this with the mindset of a buyer proactively attempting to acquire a strategic target (a company being “bought”). After completing the strategy development, evaluation of targets, and preliminary diligence, when it's time to make an offer most strategic buyers will want to lock up that target by making an attractive and preemptive offer to entice the seller to sell (as compared to continuing to build their business independently). Such an offer can also help bypass competition by compelling the seller to accept and minimizing (or eliminating) its “shopping” the interest to other potential buyers. This mindset is why the phrase “companies that receive a premium valuation are bought, not sold” is indeed the case.

The above commentary is focused on how strategic buyers acquire companies (e.g., Facebook acquiring Instagram). However, there is another important category of buyers: “financial” buyers, typically private equity firms. Rather than focusing on folding companies into their current business to achieve strategic benefits (synergies) such as leveraging existing customer bases, sales teams, and infrastructure, financial buyers look for quality businesses that they can acquire and run stand‐alone. Most private equity transactions utilize debt (“leverage”) to partially finance the transaction and use the target company's cash flow to pay it down over time. Also, PE buyers focus on more mature, thus less risky, businesses than venture investors. With these dynamics, PE buyers tend to focus on businesses with the following criteria:

Scaled revenues

Predictable revenues and cash flows

Profitable (to be able to service the debt interest and principal payments)

For businesses with the three criteria above, PE buyers present a real option. In fact, in LUMA's focus areas (digital marketing and digital media), PE buyers have been the most active buyer group. And while strategic buyers rarely buy out of processes (and instead focus on proactive acquisitions), financial buyers frequently do and often provide the engagement and competition required to arrive at interesting outcomes.

We've discussed many concepts that are important for a startup CEO to consider regarding exits. One final topic we will touch on is adviser selection. To state the obvious, your adviser should understand your business and the industry you operate in, and ideally have relationships with your potential buyers. But also note that acquisitions are extremely time‐consuming and stressful and may be the most important financial transaction of your life. Therefore, you should feel very comfortable with your adviser. Just as it is important to develop a relationship with strategic buyers, you should also develop relationships with potential advisers. Beware of advisers who push to have you sign them up immediately so they can then “add value” and/or run a process to sell your business. Instead, get to know advisers over time, leverage their networks for introductions, and see if they have any insights that can help you set your strategy or run your business. Then, when the moment comes when you need to select an adviser, hopefully you have found one that fits your selection criteria and is someone you know you can effectively work with.

Brian Andersen
Co‐Founder & Partner, LUMA Partners

Mark Greenbaum
Partner, Head of Mergers & Acquisitions

Chapter 53
Transition and Integration

The morning after your deal closes is not the time to start figuring out what comes next. No matter how hard you've been working while simultaneously running the business, managing the transaction, and managing your emotions, you have to make ample time during the transaction process to plan how you're going to “win the peace” – how you're going to transition yourself and your organization and potentially integrate into your new owner.

The Most Important Thing

How you approach the transition and integration period will obviously vary quite a bit based on the circumstance. Being acquired by a private equity firm and remaining the company's CEO will be a very different process than being acquired by a strategic buyer who keeps you intact as a business unit, which will be very different than being completely absorbed into a larger organization. Also, your personal circumstances will be very different if you have earn‐out and/or if you're staying with the new organization, you're leaving right away, or you're staying for a defined transition period. This chapter is generally written from the perspective of helping your company and your employees transition into a new operating organization, and getting ready to leave or significantly change your own role.

But fundamentally, the most important thing you can do is remember that once the deal is over, it's over. You may or may not be the CEO, but now you definitely have a new boss, and in many cases, a boss for the first time in years. “Even though the deal was called a merger,” I once heard Ted Leonsis tell the Moviefone founders a while after AOL acquired Moviefone, “please remember that you have been acquired.” Your job is to figure out how best to set your team and products up for success in the new environment, regardless of how long or short you plan to stay at the new company.

Is your role to advise? To run? To be quiet? To best serve the business and your people, you really need to ask this question of your new owners and follow their lead. And of course, as with all things, you want to do the best you can, recognizing you may not necessarily understand the buyer's motivations and plans. In fact, they may intentionally obfuscate those from you during the process. As much as you have to be selling all the way up to the closing, so do they – and if they think revealing their plans to you might cause you to walk away from the deal, they may hold them close to the vest. Having said that, it shouldn't matter. If you've taken the steps you need to prepare yourself mentally and recognize that you are no longer in control, and if you've done all you can for your people, your frame of reference should change at closing from “How can I get it closed?” to “How can I be most helpful?”

The All‐Hands

While this is probably your biggest, most important, all‐hands meeting in the history of your company, my advice is short. Speak from the heart. Do your best to “bring everyone along for the ride” even though you have had months to wrap your brain around a sale of the business, and your company will have 30 minutes. Let everyone know that regardless of whether someone will be staying or going, everyone will have a different job tomorrow. And don't forget to say thank you. A lot.

Passing the Baton

I had mixed emotions about selling the company, which is unavoidable. I was excited to see our hard work of 20 years validated with a multi‐hundred‐million‐dollar valuation. I was nervous about how people would take the news internally. I was sad knowing that some of my friends and colleagues would be let go as part of the deal. I was curious about how the buyer would handle things.

But one thing I was not mixed on was the need to put a punctuation mark – and an exclamation point, at that! – on the company's journey, especially since I knew I was going to be leaving the company shortly after the deal closed. I created a program called Passing the Baton, which the CEO of Validity graciously let me execute exactly as I wanted. The program was really about marking the end of one era and the beginning of a new one.

I never liked the idea of a standard‐issue closing dinner, where the bankers and the lawyers treat the team and the board to an expensive meal out of their deal proceeds. Those have always felt oddly inauthentic to me since external advisers, while important to the deal, generally haven't been part of the whole journey with the company. So instead of that, we opted for a series of smaller events. A few of us toured every one of our offices, held a thank‐you happy hour with the full staff, and a thank‐you dinner with leaders. These events were everything you'd expect: fun, sad, grateful, tearful. The “old timer” event we did in our largest office in Colorado was the one that hit me the hardest, seeing a room of over 50 people who had been with the company 10 ten or more years – people whom I knew well, personally, all of them.

Passing the Baton also included assembling a final “care package” for our team. We put together a special box of company logo gear made specifically for the occasion, and we made the closest thing to a senior yearbook that we could. We created a perpetual online archive of photos from throughout the company's history and asked everyone to contribute to it. We created alumni groups on LinkedIn and Facebook and invited all current employees and all alums – and by then we had over 1,300 total alums from over the years.

All of this allowed everyone in the company to say goodbye and thank you and mark the transition with fun and style.

Integration

In Chapter 30, I wrote about integrating acquisitions into your company. Integrating your company into its acquired company is the same, but in reverse. You might or might not be in charge of the process, depending on your longevity with the company post‐deal. But one thing to keep in mind is that it's no longer your company. You are there in service of the business and of your people. Whatever role you play in integration, make sure you are constantly checking in with your new owners.
