Remember, you still know a hundred times more about your business than they do, regardless of how much due diligence they did. Every time our new owners from Validity asked me a question (“Should we do X or Y,” or “Should we keep person A or person B?”), my answer was never simple. It was always, “What's your strategy with regard to Z?” and then my advice could be in context.

Finally, it's almost certain that regardless of who acquires you, you will be firing some people in connection with the transaction. Offer to your new owners that you will do this work yourself (you or your HR team). Your buyer may or may not let you, or you may or may not even still be there, but the people were your people, and this move was at least in part your decision. You owe it to them (and to yourself) to look them in the eye and explain the news yourself. And hopefully you will have taken care of them financially to the best of your abilities in terms of the deal, their options, severance, and any sale bonus, as I noted in Chapter 52, “The Sale Process.”

Chapter 54
Thinking about Your Next Step

My first rule of thumb when I was working on selling Return Path, once I realized that I would most likely be leaving the company after the deal closed, was that I wasn't going to even think about what I was going to do next until the deal actually closed. I was about 95 percent successful at achieving this, but without that conscious rule of thumb, I could easily have been sucked into both conversations and brain cycles on the topic when all of my time and energy needed to go toward running the business and closing the deal.

Downshift and Take Time Off

In order to actually get down time, you need to consciously and intentionally slow yourself down. The analogy I used on myself was that I was driving a stick shift going 100 mph, and I wanted to get into third gear. You can't downshift from 100mph into fifth to fourth to third or you'll blow up your engine. And you can't just take your foot off the gas entirely, or you'll stall. You have to slow down, then downshift, then slow down more, then downshift again.

Commit to yourself that you won't start working again for X time, or that you won't even take meetings or phone calls about new opportunities for X time. It doesn't matter what X is; some CEOs I know have taken a year or more, some just a few months. Give yourself time and space to catch up on rest and exercise and quality time with friends and family. Give yourself time and space to process what you just went through – the transaction as well as the whole entrepreneurial journey. Take a long vacation without email. Read a trashy novel and don't feel guilty about it.

Startup CEOs have a high “burden of office” – the constant presence of worries in the back or front of your mind about making the quarter, EBITDA, competition, burn rate, the fact that dozens or hundreds of employees rely on your company to pay their mortgages and feed their families. As the burden of office fades from consciousness, you will find it amazing how much more relaxed you are.

Sharpen the Saw

To borrow Stephen Covey's metaphor, one of your objectives during your time off beyond just decompressing should be to sharpen the saw, or revitalize yourself. Work different or even new muscles. Recharge your batteries. You get the idea.

The week after our deal closed, I made a list of everything I wanted to get done in my downtime. Once I got past everyone in my family rolling their eyes and saying things like “of course you have to use a spreadsheet to make a list about how to relax,” I realized there were three types of items on my list. One was personal or home admin tasks that I had either ignored or wanted to get ahead of. Two was home admin tasks that had fallen to Mariquita while I was working hard and felt like I should now take off her plate. Both of those feel – rightly so – like work, although they are all a far cry from actually working. But the third type of item on my list was “me” items, which included things like what kinds of books I wanted to read, how I wanted to take care of my physical well‐being differently both short term and long term, and things like spending more time taking guitar lessons (something I've done on and off over the years) and stone sculpture lessons (something I've never done at all but that has always interested me greatly).

As I write the second edition of this book, I am spending a lot of time sharpening the saw. Probably not as much as I would like, but more than I have done in years. It's great, it will absolutely serve its purpose of readying me to go back to work leading a new company. More important, it's making me realize that I need to do a little bit more regular sharpening of the saw day in, day out.

Telling Your Story, and the Ultimate Postmortem

I've written in Chapter 28 about the practice of doing postmortems and just how important I think they are to a learning organization and to professional growth and development. As part of all of our wrap‐up activities at the end of the Return Path journey, one of my favorite things was a 20‐year retrospective that I organized with a small number of people who had been along for most of the journey.

There were some wonderful moments in that conversation, which was structured and prepared for ahead of time and which lasted several hours. Perhaps the most poignant one was when our CTO, Shawn, said, “I feel like we dragged a $50 million business kicking and screaming against its will to be a $100 million business,” which said a lot about our market, as well as about our heart and hustle as a company.

But the best part of the postmortem was that it started me on the process of crystallizing my story, my take on what the heck I had spent the previous 20 years doing. As I embark on the next stage of my career, I feel like having a clear understanding of my startup CEO journey – the good, the bad, and the ugly – is important. And while it's important to be able to articulate it to others, it's even more important to articulate it to myself and to be able to learn from it for whatever I do next.

Next!

Once you've had your time off, once you've sharpened your saw, once you've processed what you've been through, it will be time to think about what you want to do next with your career. For some people, it may be nothing, either because they may be ready to retire and stop working age‐wise or means‐wise. If you don't fall into one of those categories, I would recommend a structured approach to thinking about what's next, because without that intentionality, as with so many things in life (and in earlier chapters of this book), you'll be funneled into a decision that may not be right for you. Certainly, if you've had a successful and high‐profile exit, you'll start getting calls right away from headhunters and venture capitalists who want you to come run another company. It's awesome and flattering – but is it right for you?

I approached this process in a way that's pretty analogous to the new idea vetting framework I laid out in Chapter 2 (funny how the very beginning and the very end have that in common). I thought about the things that were most important to me, then applied a weighting formula to them, then began running new opportunities through that framework. It proved to be very effective in helping me compare some very different opportunities: some existing companies, some raw startup, different industries, and so on.

While there's no reason to think my framework applies directly to you, sharing it may give you some ideas for creating your own when the time is right for you. For me, these were the most important elements of my next career step:

Stay in the business arena and be a CEO again (as opposed to go into academia, politics, nonprofits, etc.).

Work with awesome people, especially some former colleagues with whom I want to work with again.

Financial: Make enough money short‐term (potentially with some period of $0), still play for a large return long term, and try to do it with less risk.

Weave in a social mission of some kind: do something with a big impact on society. Make culture deeply related to the primary mission, not a secondary mission.

Sector: No MarTech or AdTech; look for a secular tailwind.

Don't enter or start a business that Google could put out of business.

Balance: Try to do it with less travel, New York primary.

Have a high degree of control over the entity/job if possible.

Play to my strengths of people‐oriented, innovating business models.

In addition to this framework, I also relied heavily on a kitchen cabinet of family members, friends, and Return Path friends to serve as a sounding board as I worked through the decision of what to do next.

As it turned out, I ended up doing two interim CEO stints in my first few months after Return Path. One was leading an interesting company in the ethics and compliance space called LRN, and the other was starting up an organization for the State of Colorado called the Innovation Response Team. Colorado Governor Jared Polis tapped me to lead the state's response to the COVID‐19 virus. We pulled together rapid‐response creative programs that required entrepreneurial, out‐of‐the‐box thinking and deep connections to the private sector (as well as cross‐agency within various levels of government), integrated with the state's Emergency Operations Center. The impact I was able to have as an interim leader of these two organizations led me and a group of former Return Path colleagues to start a new company called Bolster in partnership with High Alpha and Silicon Valley Bank. Bolster is a talent marketplace that matches companies and executives on an interim, fractional, advisory, and project basis; think of it as “NetJets for senior executives.” It will be interesting to go back to being CEO of a raw startup after 20 years and applying all the learnings from the last 20!

Conclusion

I began writing the first edition of Startup CEO about ten years into our entrepreneurial journey at Return Path. What began as a simple idea to provide email change of address services in 1999 expanded to include all sorts of products, leading one of my board members to comment that we were the world's smallest conglomerate. That wasn't a compliment! The idea behind the first edition was not to share the Return Path journey, since that's unique to us, our market, competitors, people, and culture, but to share all (or a lot of) the issues I faced as a first‐time startup CEO that other startup CEOs are likely to face.

I wrote this book for you, the first‐time startup CEO, because there's no way to learn how to be a CEO other than being one. Just because you're a first‐time CEO, seeing situations for the first time, doesn't mean that no one else has seen similar situations. And this book shares what I've learned, the mistakes I've made, and the challenges I dealt with that other first‐time CEOs will probably grapple with.

The second edition was written almost 20 years after our founding and we have now survived the dot com crisis of 2000, 9/11, the financial meltdown of 2008, and are in the throes of the global COVID‐19 pandemic today. We grew from a handful of employees to almost 500, and from virtually no revenues to over $100 million. We also faced situations that first‐time CEOs are likely to encounter as they grow, and we eventually sold the company, which I talked about in Section Six.

As a startup CEO, seeing things for the first time, you're likely to make mistakes, to fail, to get things wrong, and to feel like you don't have any (or have very little) control over outcomes. I hope I have dismissed those notions in the book, because there are a lot of things you can do both personally and organizationally to either minimize the impact of critical situations or cut them off before they even get started. One of the most important things you can do, from day one, is to create a culture that is values‐driven, to hire, promote, and fire people based on those values. I don't know what those values might be for you, but at Return Path a key one was transparency.

Another important lesson that I learned was that the business always comes first, and you have to do what's right for the business, even if that means personal hardship for you (or for those on your leadership team). It's that consistency of culture and business focus that can help you sidestep a lot of challenges, and it allows you, your team, board, and employees to make the right decisions and to do that quickly.

I found that discipline and consistency, even amidst the chaos, create a foundation on which to grow and build a sustainable company. I developed an operating system for the organization and one for me personally. At the earliest stages it's important to be consistent in communication to the company, to have quarterly (or whatever you choose) all‐hands meetings, board meetings, to schedule regular 1:1's or skip‐level meetings. It's important to me to make sure that there's enough of me at the end of the day to thoughtfully engage with my wife and children when I get home. The organizational and personal operating systems I shared will help you find and develop the discipline and consistency to improve your business.

Some of the things I dismissed early in the life of Return Path, either because I thought they were extravagant expenses or I just didn't need them, have proved to be a cornerstone of my success. Do you need an executive coach if you're just a team of three founders? Quite possibly, and the relationship you develop with an executive coach will pay dividends far beyond you. My coach works with other members of the executive team, helps facilitate executive and board retreats, and provides perspectives based on working with others. My executive assistant is not someone who just keeps my calendar, but a person who is my alter ego, can anticipate what I need, prioritize things for me, follow up on things without direction, and frees up three to four hours a day of my time.

And that's the thing about being a startup CEO: there's only one of you and there's only 24 hours in a day. Can you work 120 hours a week? Possibly, in the short‐term. Can you be effective working 120 hours a week? Probably not. The culture you create, the operating systems you develop and practice, will change both you and others and allow you to balance your life and grow your company simultaneously.

I would never claim that writing this book – or even running a company for almost two decades – has given me all the answers. The one thing I hope I can claim is that with these two experiences I've finally figured out what questions I should be asking.

My hope is that this book will inspire other startup CEOs, current or aspiring, to do the same. I wouldn't want anybody to feel that reading this book was an end in itself, any more than reading The South Beach Diet or The 4‐Hour Body would make you thin or strong. Think of me as your personal trainer and Startup CEO as a workout manual: I can set some guidelines, issue a few warnings and offer words of encouragement and advice. Now it's up to you to get to the gym and practice, practice, practice! The payoff is phenomenal—and unlike the drudgery of treadmills and weight machines, facing the challenges of running a startup day‐to‐day is a thrilling and intellectually stimulating task that never feels repetitive.

I've already quoted Fred Wilson to the effect that being a startup CEO is “lonely,” a “heavy duty,” and a “thankless task.” The number of topics I've covered in this book—and the number of times I've insisted that I'm just scratching the surface—might also suggest that being a startup CEO is one of the hardest, riskiest and most stressful jobs in the world, too. Well, it is! If you are motivated and creative, enjoy building things and enjoy being around people and creating a sense of purpose for a team, it's also the best job in the world.

## Tables

### Table 1

| Skill                                                                               | Action Items                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                |
|:------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| Communication                                                                       | Clarity in all communications, especially those with sensitive messages. Potentially run sensitive messages by others before delivering to whole group.                                                                                                     |
|                                                                                     | Continue eliminating um from vocabulary.                                                                                                                                                                                                                    |
|                                                                                     | With both the team and the board, be sure to evaluate all feedback before making value judgments or decisions, keeping more of an open mind, and note predetermining outcomes.                                                                              |
| Leadership                                                                          | Continue to demand more accountability throughout the organization – don't focus on being loved so much as being loved + respected + feared.                                                                                                                |
|                                                                                     | Consciously weave into senior staff meetings: organizational learnings, more dialogue around commitments and deadlines, and consequences of missing them, communication techniques and instruments that the senior staff has learned from Triad Consulting. |
| Job clarity                                                                         | Ensure that organizational and task ownership is appropriate, clear, and consistent.                                                                                                                                                                        |
| Support and development                                                             | Make sure people have actionable development plans and that they are making progress on those plans throughout the year.                                                                                                                                    |
|                                                                                     | See people's weaknesses more quickly … be clearer and crisper about hiring or termination decisions.                                                                                                                                                        |
|                                                                                     | Make sure to have more out‐of‐office time with New York senior staff.                                                                                                                                                                                       |
| Execution                                                                           | Ramp up personal contributions to sales and marketing execution, bringing personal priorities in line with corporate priorities.                                                                                                                            |
|                                                                                     | Spend more time with the sales force and pitching new large accounts.                                                                                                                                                                                       |
|                                                                                     | Make sure marketing is where we need it to be – keep raising the bar on output to make sure we have an extraordinary public‐facing effort.                                                                                                                  |
| Resource allocation                                                                 | Be more entrepreneurial and scrappier about running the business.                                                                                                                                                                                           |
|                                                                                     | Make sure to treat it like I'm spending my own money.                                                                                                                                                                                                       |
|                                                                                     | Solicit more outside reads on spending or cutting if I'm struggling with a particular decision.                                                                                                                                                             |
|                                                                                     | When possible, try to get some distance from the actual analytics to be able to react more to the work of others and use my gut to make decisions, rather than being too analytical personally.                                                             |
| Developmental Area                                                                  | Developmental – Action Items                                                                                                                                                                                                                                |
| Institutionalize impatience and lessen the dependency dynamic on me.                | Make others as impatient as I am for progress, success, reinvention, streamlining, and overcoming/minimizing operational realities.                                                                                                                         |
|                                                                                     | Drive deeper understanding of the core.                                                                                                                                                                                                                     |
|                                                                                     | Raise the bar on the team.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  |
|                                                                                     | Develop the team as a team.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 |
|                                                                                     | Practice relentless follow‐through.                                                                                                                                                                                                                         |
| Focus on making every staff interaction at all levels a coaching/mentoring session. | Manager feedback loops.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     |
|                                                                                     | Inquiry versus advocacy.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    |
|                                                                                     | Develop a practiced understanding of the difference between managing, coaching, and mentoring, and be able to transparently communicate to the person that this (one of these three) is the relationship/transaction that you are both engaged in.          |
|                                                                                     | Shift from providing tactical solutions to encouraging strategic thought in others.                                                                                                                                                                         |
| Continue to foster deep and sustained engagement at all levels.                     | Evolve my signature “personal touch” items to fit a larger organization, while still trying to “know” everyone: training, management training, interviewing, onboarding, 90‐day check‐ins, roundtables.                                                     |
|                                                                                     | Work with HR lead and executive assistant on technology platforms to institutionalize engagement.                                                                                                                                                           |
|                                                                                     | What's the next major cross‐functional project?                                                                                                                                                                                                             |

### Table 2

| Meeting Type                            | Time Required   | Purpose/Format                                                                           | Notes                                             |
|:----------------------------------------|:----------------|:-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:--------------------------------------------------|
| Daily check‐in                          | 5–10 minutes    | Share daily schedules and activities                                                     | Don't sit down                                    |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Keep it administrative                            |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Don't cancel even when some people can't be there |
| Weekly tactical                         | 45–90 minutes   | Review weekly activities and metrics and resolve tactical obstacles and issues           | Don't set agenda until after initial reporting    |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Postpone strategic discussions                    |
| Monthly strategic (or ad hoc strategic) | 2–4 hours       | Discuss, analyze, brainstorm, and decide on critical issues affecting long‐term success  | Limit to one or two topics                        |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Prepare and do research                           |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Engage in good conflict                           |
| Quarterly offsite review                | 1–2 days        | Review strategy, competitive landscape, industry trends, key personnel, team development | Get out of office                                 |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Focus on work; limit social activities            |
|                                         |                 |                                                                                          | Don't overstructure or overburden the schedule    |

### Table 3

| Update and maintain calendar, schedule meetings, and greet visitors   | My calendar is like a game of Sudoku. I can and do schedule my own things but Andrea handles a lot of it. She also has access to all my staff's calendars, so she can just move things around to optimize for all of us. Finally, she and I review my calendar carefully, to make sure I'm spending my time where I want to spend it.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      |
|:----------------------------------------------------------------------|:---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| Answer and screen direct phone line                                   | The bigger we get, the more vendors call me. I can't possibly take another call from a wealth management person or a real estate broker. Screening is key for this!                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        |
| Plan and coordinate company‐wide meetings and events                  | This is an extension of managing my calendar and accessing other executives' calendars – and a pretty key centralized function.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            |
| Plan and coordinate senior team offsites                              | As part of my desire as CEO to “act like you're the host of a big party,” I like this to be planned flawlessly. I do a lot of that work myself but I need a partner to drive it.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           |
| Collect and maintain confidential data                                | Every assistant I've ever had starts by swearing an oath around confidentiality.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           |
| Prepare materials for board meetings and senior team meetings         | Building Board Books is time consuming and great to offload. My executive team and I put together the table of contents, then everyone pours materials in to Andrea and – poof! – we have a Board Book. For staff meetings, she manages the standing agenda, the changes to it, and the flow of information and materials so everyone has what they need when they need it to make these meetings productive from start to finish. In our case, Andrea is part of our senior team and joins all of our meetings so she is completely up to speed on what's going on in the company. This really enables her to add value to our work. She's not just a passive participant – she's contributed great ideas over the years! |
| Coordinate and book travel (domestic and international)               | Painful and time consuming, not because Expedia is hard to use but because there is a lot of complexity and change to manage and coordinate for certain trips. While it takes a while to get an assistant up to speed on how you like to travel or how you think about travel, this is ultimately a big time saver.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        |
| Prepare expense reports                                               | You can do it but it's easier not to.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      |
| Manage staff gifts and anniversary presents for all employees         | This is a big one for me. I call every employee on their anniversary and send them a gift. Once a month, a stack of things to sign magically appears on my desk and then gets distributed.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 |
| Manage investor database                                              | I assume someday we will have a system for this. For now, IR is a function that Andrea coordinates for my CFO and me.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      |
| Assist CEO and senior team with projects as needed                    | The person in this role is really valuable in helping to organize, research, or execute major projects of any kind. Good use of time.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      |
| Prepare quarterly time analysis                                       | This is a big one for me. Every quarter, Andrea downloads my calendar and classifies all of my time, then produces an analysis showing me where I'm spending time, according to my classifications – internal, external, non‐RP, free, travel, board/investor. This really helps us plan out the next quarter so I'm intentional about where I put my hours and then it helps her manage my calendar and balance incoming requests. (For more details, see the “Feedback Loops” section in the previous chapter.)                                                                                                                                                                                                          |
| Help with communications                                              | This wasn't on Andrea's list but I'm adding it. She ends up drafting some things for me (sometimes as small as an email, sometimes – with a lot more guidance – as large as a presentation), which is very helpful. (It's always easier to edit something than create it.) I also ask Andrea to read any emails I send to “ALL” ahead of time, to make sure they make sense from someone's perspective other than my own.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  |
